<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_the_war">Opposition to the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Opposition to the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_by_Nicholas_Shepherd,_1846-crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2849" data-file-width="2249" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Abraham_Lincoln_by_Nicholas_Shepherd%2C_1846-crop.jpg/250px-Abraham_Lincoln_by_Nicholas_Shepherd%2C_1846-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Abraham_Lincoln_by_Nicholas_Shepherd%2C_1846-crop.jpg/375px-Abraham_Lincoln_by_Nicholas_Shepherd%2C_1846-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Abraham_Lincoln_by_Nicholas_Shepherd%2C_1846-crop.jpg/500px-Abraham_Lincoln_by_Nicholas_Shepherd%2C_1846-crop.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_by_Nicholas_Shepherd,_1846-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> in his late 30s as a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._House_of_Representatives" title="U.S. House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a> when he opposed the Mexican–American War. Photo taken by one of Lincoln's law students around 1846.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the United States, increasingly divided by <a href="/wiki/Sectionalism" title="Sectionalism">sectional</a> rivalry, the war was a partisan issue and an essential element in the <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="Origins of the American Civil War">origins of the American Civil War</a>. Most <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whigs</a> in the North and South opposed it;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jay166_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jay166-62">[62]</a></sup> most Democrats supported it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jay165_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jay165-63">[63]</a></sup> Southern <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrats</a>, animated by a popular belief in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manifest_Destiny" title="Manifest Destiny">Manifest Destiny</a>, supported it in hope of adding slave-owning territory to the South and avoiding being outnumbered by the faster-growing North. <a href="/wiki/John_L._O%27Sullivan" title="John L. O'Sullivan">John L. O'Sullivan</a>, editor of the <i>Democratic Review</i>, coined this phrase in its context, stating that it must be "our manifest destiny to overspread the continent allotted by Providence for the free development of our yearly multiplying millions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frederick_Douglass_by_Samuel_J_Miller,_1847-52.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1791" data-file-width="1293" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Frederick_Douglass_by_Samuel_J_Miller%2C_1847-52.png/220px-Frederick_Douglass_by_Samuel_J_Miller%2C_1847-52.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Frederick_Douglass_by_Samuel_J_Miller%2C_1847-52.png/330px-Frederick_Douglass_by_Samuel_J_Miller%2C_1847-52.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Frederick_Douglass_by_Samuel_J_Miller%2C_1847-52.png/440px-Frederick_Douglass_by_Samuel_J_Miller%2C_1847-52.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frederick_Douglass_by_Samuel_J_Miller,_1847-52.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ex-slave and powerful anti-slavery advocate <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a> (circa 1847–52) opposed the Mexican–American War.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Northern antislavery elements feared the expansion of the Southern <a href="/wiki/Slave_Power" title="Slave Power">Slave Power</a>; Whigs generally wanted to strengthen the economy with industrialization, not expand it with more land. Among the most vocal opposing the war in the House of Representatives was <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> of <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>. Adams had first voiced concerns about expanding into Mexican territory in 1836 when he opposed Texas annexation. He continued this argument in 1846 for the same reason. War with Mexico would add new slavery territory to the nation. When the vote to go to war with Mexico came to a vote on May 13, Adams spoke a resounding "NO" in the chamber. Only 13 others followed his lead.</p>
<p>Ex-slave <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a> opposed the war and was dismayed by the weakness of the anti-war movement. "The determination of our slave holding president, and the probability of his success in wringing from the people, men and money to carry it on, is made evident by the puny opposition arrayed against him. None seem willing to take their stand for peace at all risks."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Democrats wanted more land; northern Democrats were attracted by the possibilities in the far northwest. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joshua_Giddings" title="Joshua Giddings">Joshua Giddings</a> led a group of dissenters in Washington D.C. He called the war with Mexico "an aggressive, unholy, and unjust war", and voted against supplying soldiers and weapons. He said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In the murder of Mexicans upon their own soil, or in robbing them of their country, I can take no part either now or hereafter. The guilt of these crimes must rest on others. I will not participate in them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Fellow Whig <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> contested the causes for the war and demanded to know exactly where Thornton had been attacked and American blood shed. "Show me the spot", he demanded. In the Senate <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Corwin" title="Thomas Corwin">Thomas Corwin</a>, Whig of Ohio, gave a long speech indicting presidential war in 1847.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Whig leader <a href="/wiki/Robert_Toombs" title="Robert Toombs">Robert Toombs</a> of Georgia declared:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>This war is nondescript.... We charge the President with usurping the war-making power ... with seizing a country ... which had been for centuries, and was then in the possession of the Mexicans.... Let us put a check upon this lust of dominion. We had territory enough, Heaven knew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VII._Rowse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3296" data-file-width="2391" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/VII._Rowse.jpg/220px-VII._Rowse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/VII._Rowse.jpg/330px-VII._Rowse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/VII._Rowse.jpg/440px-VII._Rowse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VII._Rowse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a> who spent a night in jail for not paying poll taxes to support the war and later wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Civil_Disobedience_(Thoreau)" title="Civil Disobedience (Thoreau)">Civil Disobedience</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Northern <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionists</a> attacked the war as an attempt by slave-owners to strengthen the grip of slavery and thus ensure their continued influence in the federal government. Acting on his convictions, <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a> was jailed for his refusal to pay taxes to support the war, and penned his famous essay <i><a href="/wiki/Civil_Disobedience_(Thoreau)" title="Civil Disobedience (Thoreau)">Civil Disobedience</a></i>.</p>
<p>Democratic Congressman <a href="/wiki/David_Wilmot" title="David Wilmot">David Wilmot</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Wilmot_Proviso" title="Wilmot Proviso">Wilmot Proviso</a>, which aimed to prohibit slavery in new territory acquired from Mexico. Wilmot's proposal did not pass Congress, but it spurred further hostility between the factions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defense_of_the_war">Defense of the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Defense of the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Besides alleging that the actions of Mexican military forces within the disputed boundary lands north of the Rio Grande constituted an attack on American soil, the war's advocates viewed the territories of New Mexico and California as only nominally Mexican possessions with very tenuous ties to Mexico. They saw the territories as actually unsettled, ungoverned, and unprotected frontier lands, whose non-aboriginal population, where there was any at all, represented a substantial—in places even a majority—American component. Moreover, the territories were feared to be under imminent threat of acquisition by America's rival on the continent, the British.</p>
<p>President Polk reprised these arguments in his Third Annual Message to Congress on December 7, 1847.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In it he scrupulously detailed his administration's position on the origins of the conflict, the measures the U.S. had taken to avoid hostilities, and the justification for declaring war. He also elaborated upon the many outstanding financial claims by American citizens against Mexico and argued that, in view of the country's insolvency, the cession of some large portion of its northern territories was the only indemnity realistically available as compensation. This helped to rally congressional Democrats to his side, ensuring passage of his war measures and bolstering support for the war in the U.S.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._press_and_popular_war_enthusiasm">U.S. press and popular war enthusiasm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: U.S. press and popular war enthusiasm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:War_News_from_Mexico.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1966" data-file-width="1779" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/War_News_from_Mexico.jpg/290px-War_News_from_Mexico.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/War_News_from_Mexico.jpg/435px-War_News_from_Mexico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/War_News_from_Mexico.jpg/580px-War_News_from_Mexico.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:War_News_from_Mexico.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>War News from Mexico</i> (1848)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Whipping up support for the war was embraced by the press. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Caton_Woodville" title="Richard Caton Woodville">Richard Caton Woodville</a>'s lithograph, <i>War News From Mexico</i> satirizes the reactions of various segments of the American population to the war. The lithograph is generally considered unfavorable to the war's apparent pro-slavery motivations. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The coverage of the war was an important development in the U.S., with journalists as well as letter-writing soldiers giving the public in the U.S. "their first-ever independent news coverage of warfare from home or abroad."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> During the war, inventions such as the telegraph created new means of communication that updated people with the latest news from the reporters, who were on the scene. The most important of these was <a href="/wiki/George_Wilkins_Kendall" title="George Wilkins Kendall">George Wilkins Kendall</a>, a Northerner who wrote for the New Orleans <i>Picayune</i>, and whose collected <i>Dispatches from the Mexican War</i> constitute an important primary source for the conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> With more than a decade's experience reporting urban crime, the "penny press" realized the public's voracious demand for astounding war news. Moreover, Shelley Streetby demonstrates that the print revolution (1830s-1840s), which preceded the U.S.-Mexican War, made it possible for the distribution of cheap newspapers throughout the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> This was the first time in American history that accounts by journalists, instead of opinions of politicians, had great influence in shaping people's opinions about and attitudes toward a war. Along with written accounts of the war, there were war artists giving a visual dimension to the war at the time and immediately afterward. <a href="/wiki/Carl_Nebel" title="Carl Nebel">Carl Nebel</a>'s visual depictions of the war are well known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>By getting constant reports from the battlefield, Americans became emotionally united as a community. News about the war always caused extraordinary popular excitement. In the Spring of 1846, news about <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a>'s victory at Palo Alto brought up a large crowd that met in a cotton textile town of Lowell, Massachusetts. New York celebrated the twin victories at Veracruz and Buena Vista in May 1847. Among fireworks and illuminations, they had a "grand procession" of about 400,000 people.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Generals Taylor and Scott became heroes for their people and later became presidential candidates.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conduct_of_the_war">Conduct of the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Conduct of the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War_campaigns" title="Mexican–American War campaigns">Mexican–American War campaigns</a></div>
<p>After the declaration of war on May 13, 1846, U.S. forces invaded Mexican territory on two main fronts. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War" title="United States Department of War">U.S. War Department</a> sent a U.S. Cavalry force under <a href="/wiki/Stephen_W._Kearny" title="Stephen W. Kearny">Stephen W. Kearny</a> to invade western Mexico from <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Barracks_Military_Post" title="Jefferson Barracks Military Post">Jefferson Barracks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fort_Leavenworth" title="Fort Leavenworth">Fort Leavenworth</a>, reinforced by a Pacific fleet under <a href="/wiki/John_D._Sloat" title="John D. Sloat">John D. Sloat</a>. This was done primarily because of concerns that Britain might also try to seize the area. Two more forces, one under <a href="/wiki/John_E._Wool" title="John E. Wool">John E. Wool</a> and the other under Taylor, were ordered to occupy Mexico as far south as the city of <a href="/wiki/Monterrey" title="Monterrey">Monterrey</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Mexico_campaign">New Mexico campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: New Mexico campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Santa_Fe" title="Capture of Santa Fe">Capture of Santa Fe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taos_Revolt" title="Taos Revolt">Taos Revolt</a></div>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kearny-Las-Vegas-Aug-1846-engraving-1882.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="428" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Kearny-Las-Vegas-Aug-1846-engraving-1882.jpg/170px-Kearny-Las-Vegas-Aug-1846-engraving-1882.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Kearny-Las-Vegas-Aug-1846-engraving-1882.jpg/255px-Kearny-Las-Vegas-Aug-1846-engraving-1882.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Kearny-Las-Vegas-Aug-1846-engraving-1882.jpg/340px-Kearny-Las-Vegas-Aug-1846-engraving-1882.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kearny-Las-Vegas-Aug-1846-engraving-1882.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gen. Kearny's <a href="/wiki/Annexation" title="Annexation">annexation</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, August 15, 1846</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General" title="General">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_W._Kearny" title="Stephen W. Kearny">Stephen W. Kearny</a> moved southwest from <a href="/wiki/Fort_Leavenworth" title="Fort Leavenworth">Fort Leavenworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a> with about 1,700 men in his <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_West_(1846)" title="Army of the West (1846)">Army of the West</a>. Kearny's orders were to secure the territories <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_de_Nuevo_M%C3%A9xico" title="Santa Fe de Nuevo México">Nuevo México</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alta_California" title="Alta California">Alta California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoSF_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-BoSF-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Santa Fe, Governor <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Armijo" title="Manuel Armijo">Manuel Armijo</a> wanted to avoid battle, but on August 9, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_priest" title="Catholic priest">Catholic priests</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diego_Archuleta" title="Diego Archuleta">Diego Archuleta</a> (the young regular-army commander), and the young militia officers <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Antonio_Chaves" title="Manuel Antonio Chaves">Manuel Chaves</a> and Miguel Pino forced him to muster a defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simmons_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons-76">[76]</a></sup> Armijo set up a position in Apache Canyon, a narrow pass about 10 miles (16 km) southeast of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> However, on August 14, before the American army was even in view, he decided not to fight. (An American named James Magoffin claimed he had convinced Armijo and Archuleta to follow this course;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keleher_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keleher-78">[78]</a></sup> an unverified story says he bribed Armijo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beck_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beck-79">[79]</a></sup>) When Pino, Chaves, and some of the militiamen insisted on fighting, Armijo ordered the cannon pointed at them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simmons_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons-76">[76]</a></sup> The New Mexican army retreated to Santa Fe, and Armijo fled to <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_(state)" title="Chihuahua (state)">Chihuahua</a>.</p>
<p>Kearny and his troops encountered no Mexican forces when they arrived on August 15. Kearny and his force entered Santa Fe and claimed the New Mexico Territory for the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> without a shot being fired. Kearny declared himself the military governor of the <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico_Territory" title="New Mexico Territory">New Mexico Territory</a> on August 18 and established a civilian government. He then took the remainder of his army west to <a href="/wiki/Alta_California" title="Alta California">Alta California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoSF_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-BoSF-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Kearny departed with his forces for <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, he left Colonel <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Price" title="Sterling Price">Sterling Price</a> in command of U.S. forces in New Mexico. He appointed <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bent" title="Charles Bent">Charles Bent</a> as New Mexico's first territorial governor.</p>
<p>Following Kearny's departure, dissenters in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe,_New_Mexico" title="Santa Fe, New Mexico">Santa Fe</a> plotted a <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a> uprising. When the plans were discovered by the US authorities, the dissenters postponed the uprising. They attracted numerous Indian allies, including <a href="/wiki/Puebloan_peoples" title="Puebloan peoples">Puebloan peoples</a>, who also wanted to push the Americans from the territory. On the morning of January 19, 1847, the insurrectionists began the revolt in Don Fernando de Taos, present-day <a href="/wiki/Taos,_New_Mexico" title="Taos, New Mexico">Taos, New Mexico</a>, which later gave it the name the <a href="/wiki/Taos_Revolt" title="Taos Revolt">Taos Revolt</a>. They were led by <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Montoya" title="Pablo Montoya">Pablo Montoya</a>, a New Mexican, and <a href="/wiki/Tom%C3%A1s_Romero" title="Tomás Romero">Tomás Romero</a>, a Taos <a href="/wiki/Puebloan_peoples" title="Puebloan peoples">pueblo Indian</a> also known as <i>Tomasito</i> (Little Thomas).</p>
<p>Romero led an Indian force to the house of Governor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bent" title="Charles Bent">Charles Bent</a>, where they broke down the door, shot Bent with arrows, and scalped him in front of his family. They moved on, leaving Bent still alive. With his wife Ignacia and children, and the wives of friends <a href="/wiki/Kit_Carson" title="Kit Carson">Kit Carson</a> and Thomas Boggs, the group escaped by digging through the adobe walls of their house into the one next door. When the insurgents discovered the party, they killed Bent, but left the women and children unharmed.</p>
<p>The next day a large armed force of approximately 500 New Mexicans and Pueblo attacked and laid siege to Simeon Turley's mill in <a href="/wiki/Arroyo_Hondo,_New_Mexico" title="Arroyo Hondo, New Mexico">Arroyo Hondo</a>, several miles outside of Taos. Charles Autobees, an employee at the mill, saw the men coming. He rode to <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe,_New_Mexico" title="Santa Fe, New Mexico">Santa Fe</a> for help from the occupying US forces. Eight to ten <a href="/wiki/Mountain_man" title="Mountain man">mountain men</a> were left at the mill for defense. After a day-long battle, only two of the mountain men, <a href="/wiki/John_David_Albert" title="John David Albert">John David Albert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Tate_Tobin" title="Thomas Tate Tobin">Thomas Tate Tobin</a>, Autobees' half brother, survived. Both escaped separately on foot during the night. The same day New Mexican insurgents killed seven American traders who were passing through the village of <a href="/wiki/Mora,_New_Mexico" title="Mora, New Mexico">Mora</a>. At most, 15 Americans were killed in both actions on January 20.</p>
<p>The US military moved quickly to quash the revolt; Col. Price led more than 300 U.S. troops from Santa Fe to Taos, together with 65 volunteers, including a few New Mexicans, organized by <a href="/wiki/Ceran_St._Vrain" title="Ceran St. Vrain">Ceran St. Vrain</a>, the business partner of the brothers William and Charles Bent. Along the way, the combined forces beat back a force of some 1,500 New Mexicans and Pueblo at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ca%C3%B1ada" title="Battle of Cañada">Santa Cruz de la Cañada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Embudo_Pass" title="Battle of Embudo Pass">Embudo Pass</a>. The insurgents retreated to <a href="/wiki/Taos_Pueblo" title="Taos Pueblo">Taos Pueblo</a>, where they took refuge in the thick-walled adobe church.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Pueblo_de_Taos" title="Siege of Pueblo de Taos">ensuing battle</a>, the US breached a wall of the church and directed cannon fire into the interior, inflicting many casualties and killing about 150 rebels. They captured 400 more men after close hand-to-hand fighting. Only seven Americans died in the battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrard_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garrard-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>A separate force of US troops under captains <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Israel_R._Hendley&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Israel R. Hendley (page does not exist)">Israel R. Hendley</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jesse_I._Morin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jesse I. Morin (page does not exist)">Jesse I. Morin</a> campaigned against the rebels in Mora. The <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Mora" title="First Battle of Mora">First Battle of Mora</a> ended in a New Mexican victory. The Americans attacked again in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Mora" title="Second Battle of Mora">Second Battle of Mora</a> and won, which ended their operations against Mora. New Mexican rebels engaged US forces three more times in the following months. The actions are known as the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Canyon_Affair" title="Red River Canyon Affair">Battle of Red River Canyon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_Affair" title="Las Vegas Affair">Battle of Las Vegas</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cienega_Affair" title="Cienega Affair">Battle of Cienega Creek</a>. After the US forces won each battle, the New Mexicans and Indians ended open warfare.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>American officers with a background in law drew up a temporary legal system for the territory called <a href="/wiki/Kearny_Code" title="Kearny Code">Kearny Code</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="California_campaign">California campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: California campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_California" title="Conquest of California">Conquest of California</a></div>
<div aria-labelledby="California_Campaign" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">

<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Campaignbox_California_Campaign" title="Template talk:Campaignbox California Campaign"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Campaignbox_California_Campaign&amp;action=edit"><abbr style=";background-color:#B0C4DE;;background:none transparent;border:none;" title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

</div>
<div id="California_Campaign" style="font-size:114%"><span style="line-height:1.6em"><a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_California" title="Conquest of California">California Campaign</a></span></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Original_Todd_bear_flag.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="510" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Original_Todd_bear_flag.jpg/250px-Original_Todd_bear_flag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Original_Todd_bear_flag.jpg/375px-Original_Todd_bear_flag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Original_Todd_bear_flag.jpg/500px-Original_Todd_bear_flag.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Original_Todd_bear_flag.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A replica of the first "Bear Flag" now at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presidio_of_Sonoma" title="Presidio of Sonoma">El Presidio de Sonoma</a>, or <i>Sonoma Barracks</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the U.S. declared war against Mexico on May 13, 1846,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> it took almost three months (until early August 1846) for definitive word of Congress' declaration of war to get to California. American consul <a href="/wiki/Thomas_O._Larkin" title="Thomas O. Larkin">Thomas O. Larkin</a>, stationed in <a href="/wiki/Monterey,_California" title="Monterey, California">Monterey</a>, worked successfully during the events in that vicinity to avoid bloodshed between Americans and the Mexican military garrison commanded by General <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Castro" title="José Castro">José Castro</a>, the senior military officer in California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Captain <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont" title="John C. Frémont">John C. Frémont</a>, leading a U.S. Army topographical expedition to survey the <a href="/wiki/Great_Basin" title="Great Basin">Great Basin</a>, entered the Sacramento Valley in December 1845.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Frémont's party was at Upper Klamath Lake, Oregon Territory, when it received word that war between Mexico and the U.S. was imminent;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> the party then returned to California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mexico had issued a proclamation that unnaturalized foreigners were no longer permitted to have land in California and were subject to expulsion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> With rumors swirling that General Castro was massing an army against them, American settlers in the Sacramento Valley banded together to meet the threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> On June 14, 1846, 34 American settlers seized control of the undefended Mexican government outpost of <a href="/wiki/Sonoma,_California" title="Sonoma, California">Sonoma</a> to forestall Castro's plans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> One settler created the Bear Flag and raised it over Sonoma Plaza. Within a week, 70 more volunteers joined the rebels' force,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> which grew to nearly 300 in early July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> This event, led by <a href="/wiki/William_B._Ide" title="William B. Ide">William B. Ide</a>, became known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bear_Flag_Revolt" title="Bear Flag Revolt">Bear Flag Revolt</a>.</p>
<p>On June 25, Frémont's party arrived to assist in an expected military confrontation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, then called Yerba Buena, was occupied by the Bear Flaggers on July 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> On July 5 Frémont's <a href="/wiki/California_Battalion" title="California Battalion">California Battalion</a> was formed by combining his forces with many of the rebels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Commodore <a href="/wiki/John_D._Sloat" title="John D. Sloat">John D. Sloat</a>, commander of the U.S. Navy's Pacific Squadron, near Mazatlan, Mexico, had received orders to seize San Francisco Bay and blockade California ports when he was positive that war had begun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Sloat set sail for Monterey, reaching it on July 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Sloat, upon hearing of the events in Sonoma and Frémont's involvement, erroneously believed Frémont to be acting on orders from Washington and ordered his forces to occupy Monterey on July 7 and raise the American flag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 9, 70 sailors and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">marines</a> landed at Yerba Buena and raised the American flag. Later that day in Sonoma, the Bear Flag was lowered and the American flag was raised in its place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>On Sloat's orders, Frémont brought 160 volunteers to Monterey, in addition to the California Battalion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> On July 15, Sloat transferred his command of the Pacific Squadron to Commodore <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Stockton" title="Robert F. Stockton">Robert F. Stockton</a>, who was more militarily aggressive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_p._154_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker_p._154-100">[100]</a></sup> He mustered the willing members of the California Battalion into military service with Frémont in command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_p._154_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walker_p._154-100">[100]</a></sup> Stockton ordered Frémont to <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a> to prepare to move northward to <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> As Frémont landed, Stockton's 360 men arrived in San Pedro.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> General Castro and Governor <a href="/wiki/P%C3%ADo_Pico" title="Pío Pico">Pío Pico</a> wrote farewells and fled separately to the Mexican state of Sonora.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stockton's army entered Los Angeles unopposed on August 13, whereupon he sent a report to the Secretary of State that "California is entirely free from Mexican dominion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Stockton, however, left a tyrannical officer in charge of Los Angeles with a small force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Californio" title="Californio">Californios</a></i> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Flores" title="José María Flores">José María Flores</a>, acting on their own and without federal help from Mexico, in the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Los_Angeles" title="Siege of Los Angeles">Siege of Los Angeles</a>, forced the American garrison to retreat on September 29.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> They also forced small U.S. garrisons in San Diego and Santa Barbara to flee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Captain William Mervine landed 350 sailors and marines at San Pedro on October 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> They were ambushed and repulsed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dominguez_Rancho" title="Battle of Dominguez Rancho">Battle of Dominguez Rancho</a> by Flores' forces in less than an hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Four Americans died, with 8 severely injured. Stockton arrived with reinforcements at San Pedro, which increased the American forces there to 800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> He and Mervine then set up a base of operations at San Diego.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, U.S. Colonel <a href="/wiki/Stephen_W._Kearny" title="Stephen W. Kearny">Stephen W. Kearny</a> and his force of about 100 men, who had performed a grueling march across <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sonoran_Desert" title="Sonoran Desert">Sonoran Desert</a>, crossed the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_River" title="Colorado River">Colorado River</a> in late November, 1846.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Stockton sent a 35-man patrol from San Diego to meet them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> On December 7, 100 <a href="/wiki/Lancer" title="Lancer">lancers</a> under General <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Pico" title="Andrés Pico">Andrés Pico</a> (brother of the governor), tipped off and lying in wait, fought Kearny's army of about 150 at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_San_Pasqual" title="Battle of San Pasqual">Battle of San Pasqual</a>, where 22 of Kearny's men (one of whom later died of wounds), including three officers, were killed in 30 minutes of fighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The wounded Kearny and his bloodied force pushed on until they had to establish a defensive position on "Mule Hill".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> However, General Pico kept the hill under siege for four days until a 215-man American relief force arrived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frémont and the 428-man California Battalion arrived in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Luis_Obispo" title="San Luis Obispo">San Luis Obispo</a> on December 14<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> and Santa Barbara on December 27.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> On December 28, a 600-man American force under Kearny began a 150-mile march to Los Angeles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Flores then moved his ill-equipped 500-man force to a 50-foot-high bluff above the <a href="/wiki/San_Gabriel_River_(California)" title="San Gabriel River (California)">San Gabriel River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> On January 8, 1847, the Stockton-Kearny army defeated the Californio force in the two-hour <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Rio_San_Gabriel" title="Battle of Rio San Gabriel">Battle of Rio San Gabriel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> That same day, Frémont's force arrived at San Fernando.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> The next day, January 9, the Stockton-Kearny forces fought and won the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_La_Mesa" title="Battle of La Mesa">Battle of La Mesa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> On January 10, the U.S. Army entered Los Angeles to no resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 12, Frémont and two of Pico's officers agreed to terms for a surrender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_p._246_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker_p._246-127">[127]</a></sup> Articles of Capitulation were signed on January 13 by Frémont, Andrés Pico and six others at a <a href="/wiki/Ranch" title="Ranch"><i>rancho</i></a> at <a href="/wiki/Cahuenga_Pass" title="Cahuenga Pass">Cahuenga Pass</a> (modern-day North Hollywood).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_p._246_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walker_p._246-127">[127]</a></sup> This became known as the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Cahuenga" title="Treaty of Cahuenga">Treaty of Cahuenga</a>, which marked the end of armed resistance in California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_p._246_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-Walker_p._246-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<div aria-labelledby="Pacific_Coast_Campaign" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="margin:0;float:right;clear:right;width:315px;margin-bottom:0.5em;margin-left:1em;;padding:3px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacific_Coast_campaign">Pacific Coast campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Pacific Coast campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Coast_Campaign" title="Pacific Coast Campaign">Pacific Coast Campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_and_mexican_soldiers_1846-1848.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4288" data-file-width="2848" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/US_and_mexican_soldiers_1846-1848.jpg/220px-US_and_mexican_soldiers_1846-1848.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/US_and_mexican_soldiers_1846-1848.jpg/330px-US_and_mexican_soldiers_1846-1848.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/US_and_mexican_soldiers_1846-1848.jpg/440px-US_and_mexican_soldiers_1846-1848.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_and_mexican_soldiers_1846-1848.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
American (left) and Mexican (right) uniforms of the period.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/USS_Independence_(1814)" title="USS Independence (1814)">USS <i>Independence</i></a> assisted in the blockade of the Mexican Pacific coast, capturing the Mexican ship <i>Correo</i> and a <a href="/wiki/Launch_(boat)" title="Launch (boat)">launch</a> on May 16, 1847. She supported the capture of <a href="/wiki/Guaymas" title="Guaymas">Guaymas</a>, Sonora, on October 19, 1847, and landed bluejackets and Marines to occupy <a href="/wiki/Mazatl%C3%A1n" title="Mazatlán">Mazatlán</a>, Sinaloa, on November 11, 1847. After upper California was secure, most of the Pacific Squadron proceeded down the California coast, capturing all major cities of the <a href="/wiki/Baja_California_Territory" title="Baja California Territory">Baja California Territory</a> and capturing or destroying nearly all Mexican vessels in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_California" title="Gulf of California">Gulf of California</a>. Other ports, not on the peninsula, were taken as well. The objective of the Pacific Coast Campaign was to capture <a href="/wiki/Mazatl%C3%A1n" title="Mazatlán">Mazatlán</a>, on the Mexican mainland, which was a major supply base for Mexican forces. Numerous Mexican ships were also captured by this squadron, with the USS <i>Cyane</i> given credit for 18 ships captured and numerous destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Entering the Gulf of California, <i>Independence</i>, <i>Congress</i>, and <i>Cyane</i> seized <a href="/wiki/La_Paz,_Baja_California_Sur" title="La Paz, Baja California Sur">La Paz</a>, then captured and burned the small Mexican fleet at <a href="/wiki/Guaymas" title="Guaymas">Guaymas</a>. Within a month, they cleared the Gulf of hostile ships, destroying or capturing 30 vessels. Later, their sailors and marines captured the port of Mazatlán on November 11, 1847. A Mexican campaign under <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Pineda_Munoz" title="Manuel Pineda Munoz">Manuel Pineda Muñoz</a> to retake the various captured ports resulted in several small clashes (<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mulege" title="Battle of Mulege">Battle of Mulege</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_La_Paz" title="Battle of La Paz">Battle of La Paz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_San_Jos%C3%A9_del_Cabo" title="Battle of San José del Cabo">Battle of San José del Cabo</a>) and two sieges (<a href="/wiki/Siege_of_La_Paz" title="Siege of La Paz">Siege of La Paz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_San_Jos%C3%A9_del_Cabo" title="Siege of San José del Cabo">Siege of San José del Cabo</a>) in which the Pacific Squadron ships provided artillery support. U.S. garrisons remained in control of the ports.</p>
<p>Following reinforcement, Lt. Col. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_S._Burton" title="Henry S. Burton">Henry S. Burton</a> marched out. His forces rescued captured Americans, captured Pineda, and, on March 31, defeated and dispersed remaining Mexican forces at the <a href="/wiki/Skirmish_of_Todos_Santos" title="Skirmish of Todos Santos">Skirmish of Todos Santos</a>, unaware that the Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo had been signed in February 1848 and a truce agreed to on March 6. When the American garrisons were evacuated to Monterey following the treaty ratification, many Mexicans went with them: those who had supported the American cause and had thought Lower California would also be annexed along with Upper California.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northeastern_Mexico">Northeastern Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Northeastern Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:General_Zachary_Taylor_rides_his_horse_at_Palo_Alto_Battle_-_May_8,_1846.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/General_Zachary_Taylor_rides_his_horse_at_Palo_Alto_Battle_-_May_8%2C_1846.JPG/320px-General_Zachary_Taylor_rides_his_horse_at_Palo_Alto_Battle_-_May_8%2C_1846.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/General_Zachary_Taylor_rides_his_horse_at_Palo_Alto_Battle_-_May_8%2C_1846.JPG/480px-General_Zachary_Taylor_rides_his_horse_at_Palo_Alto_Battle_-_May_8%2C_1846.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/General_Zachary_Taylor_rides_his_horse_at_Palo_Alto_Battle_-_May_8%2C_1846.JPG/640px-General_Zachary_Taylor_rides_his_horse_at_Palo_Alto_Battle_-_May_8%2C_1846.JPG 2x" width="320"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:General_Zachary_Taylor_rides_his_horse_at_Palo_Alto_Battle_-_May_8,_1846.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a> rides his white horse at the Battle of Palo Alto near present-day <a href="/wiki/Brownsville,_Texas" title="Brownsville, Texas">Brownsville</a> – May 8, 1846</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Mexican Army's defeats at Palo Alto and Resaca de la Palma caused political turmoil in Mexico, turmoil which <a href="/wiki/Antonio_L%C3%B3pez_de_Santa_Anna" title="Antonio López de Santa Anna">Antonio López de Santa Anna</a> used to revive his political career and return from self-imposed exile in Cuba in mid-August 1846.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> It was President Polk's plan to bring back the exiled dictator who had defeated the Texans at the Alamo and <a href="/wiki/Goliad_massacre" title="Goliad massacre">Goliad</a>. On 4 August 1846, "Polk negotiated a deal to not only bring Santa Anna back, but to pay him $2 million—ostensibly a bribe as an advance payment on the cession of California."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Santa Anna promised the U.S. that if allowed to pass through the blockade, he would negotiate a peaceful conclusion to the war and sell the New Mexico and Alta California territories to the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Once Santa Anna arrived in Mexico City, however, he reneged on his deal with the U.S. and offered his services to the Mexican government. Then, after being appointed commanding general, he reneged again and seized the presidency.</p>
<p>Led by Zachary Taylor, 2,300 U.S. troops crossed the Rio Grande after some initial difficulties in obtaining river transport. His soldiers occupied the city of <a href="/wiki/Matamoros,_Tamaulipas" title="Matamoros, Tamaulipas">Matamoros</a>, then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camargo,_Tamaulipas" title="Camargo, Tamaulipas">Camargo</a> (where the soldiery suffered the first of many problems with disease) and then proceeded south and besieged the city of <a href="/wiki/Monterrey" title="Monterrey">Monterrey</a>. The hard-fought <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monterrey" title="Battle of Monterrey">Battle of Monterrey</a> resulted in serious losses on both sides. The American light artillery was ineffective against the stone fortifications of the city. The Mexican forces were under General <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Ampudia" title="Pedro de Ampudia">Pedro de Ampudia</a> and repulsed Taylor's best infantry division at Fort Teneria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urban_Warfare_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urban_Warfare-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nebel_Mexican_War_02_Capture_of_Monterrey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3769" data-file-width="5131" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Nebel_Mexican_War_02_Capture_of_Monterrey.jpg/250px-Nebel_Mexican_War_02_Capture_of_Monterrey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Nebel_Mexican_War_02_Capture_of_Monterrey.jpg/375px-Nebel_Mexican_War_02_Capture_of_Monterrey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Nebel_Mexican_War_02_Capture_of_Monterrey.jpg/500px-Nebel_Mexican_War_02_Capture_of_Monterrey.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nebel_Mexican_War_02_Capture_of_Monterrey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monterrey" title="Battle of Monterrey">Battle of Monterrey</a> September 20–24, 1846, after a painting by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Nebel" title="Carl Nebel">Carl Nebel</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nebel_Mexican_War_03_Battle_of_Buena_Vista.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3787" data-file-width="5192" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Nebel_Mexican_War_03_Battle_of_Buena_Vista.jpg/250px-Nebel_Mexican_War_03_Battle_of_Buena_Vista.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Nebel_Mexican_War_03_Battle_of_Buena_Vista.jpg/375px-Nebel_Mexican_War_03_Battle_of_Buena_Vista.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Nebel_Mexican_War_03_Battle_of_Buena_Vista.jpg/500px-Nebel_Mexican_War_03_Battle_of_Buena_Vista.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nebel_Mexican_War_03_Battle_of_Buena_Vista.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Buena_Vista" title="Battle of Buena Vista">Battle of Buena Vista</a>, February 23, 1847</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>American soldiers, including many West Pointers, had never engaged in urban warfare before and they marched straight down the open streets, where they were annihilated by Mexican defenders well-hidden in Monterrey's thick adobe homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urban_Warfare_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Urban_Warfare-132">[132]</a></sup> Two days later, they changed their urban warfare tactics. Texan soldiers had fought in a Mexican city before (the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_B%C3%A9xar" title="Siege of Béxar">Siege of Béxar</a> in December 1835) and advised Taylor's generals that the Americans needed to "<a href="/wiki/Mouse-holing" title="Mouse-holing">mouse hole</a>" through the city's homes. In other words, they needed to punch holes in the side or roofs of the homes and fight hand to hand inside the structures. Mexicans called the Texas soldiers the Diabólicos Tejanos (the Devil Texans).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> This method proved successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Eventually, these actions drove and trapped Ampudia's men into the city's central plaza, where howitzer shelling forced Ampudia to negotiate. Taylor agreed to allow the Mexican Army to evacuate and to an eight-week armistice in return for the surrender of the city. Under pressure from Washington, Taylor broke the armistice and occupied the city of Saltillo, southwest of Monterrey. Santa Anna blamed the loss of Monterrey and Saltillo on Ampudia and demoted him to command a small artillery battalion.</p>
<p>On February 22, 1847, Santa Anna personally marched north to fight Taylor with 20,000 men. Taylor, with 4,600 men, had entrenched at a mountain pass called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Buena_Vista,_Coahuila,_Mexico&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Buena Vista, Coahuila, Mexico (page does not exist)">Buena Vista</a>. Santa Anna suffered desertions on the way north and arrived with 15,000 men in a tired state. He demanded and was refused surrender of the U.S. Army; he attacked the next morning. Santa Anna flanked the U.S. positions by sending his cavalry and some of his infantry up the steep terrain that made up one side of the pass, while a division of infantry attacked frontally along the road leading to Buena Vista. Furious fighting ensued, during which the U.S troops were nearly routed, but managed to cling to their entrenched position, thanks to the Mississippi Rifles, a volunteer regiment led by <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a>, who formed them into a defensive V formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The Mexicans had inflicted considerable losses but Santa Anna had gotten word of upheaval in Mexico City, so he withdrew that night, leaving Taylor in control of part of Northern Mexico.</p>
<p>Polk mistrusted Taylor, who he felt had shown incompetence in the Battle of Monterrey by agreeing to the armistice, and may have considered him a political rival for the White House. Taylor later used the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Buena_Vista" title="Battle of Buena Vista">Battle of Buena Vista</a> as the centerpiece of his successful 1848 presidential campaign.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northwestern_Mexico">Northwestern Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Northwestern Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Treaty of Bear Springs ended a large scale insurrection by the <a href="/wiki/Ute_people" title="Ute people">Ute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zuni_people" title="Zuni people">Zuni</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moquis" title="Moquis">Moquis</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navajo_people" title="Navajo people">Navajo</a> tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> After the successful conquest of New Mexico, American troops moved into modern-day northwest Mexico.</p>
<p>On March 1, 1847, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_William_Doniphan" title="Alexander William Doniphan">Alexander W. Doniphan</a> occupied <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_City" title="Chihuahua City">Chihuahua City</a>. British consul John Potts did not want to let Doniphan search Governor Trias's mansion, and unsuccessfully asserted it was under British protection. American merchants in Chihuahua wanted the American force to stay in order to protect their business. <a href="/wiki/William_Gilpin_(governor)" title="William Gilpin (governor)">Major William Gilpin</a> advocated a march on Mexico City and convinced a majority of officers, but Doniphan subverted this plan. Then in late April, Taylor ordered the First Missouri Mounted Volunteers to leave Chihuahua and join him at Saltillo. The American merchants either followed or returned to Santa Fe. Along the way, the townspeople of <a href="/wiki/Parras" title="Parras">Parras</a> enlisted Doniphan's aid against an Indian raiding party that had taken children, horses, mules, and money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>The civilian population of northern Mexico offered little resistance to the American invasion, possibly because the country had already been devastated by <a href="/wiki/Comanche" title="Comanche">Comanche</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apache" title="Apache">Apache</a> Indian raids. <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Gregg" title="Josiah Gregg">Josiah Gregg</a>, who was with the American army in northern Mexico, said that "the whole country from New Mexico to the borders of Durango is almost entirely depopulated. The haciendas and ranchos have been mostly abandoned, and the people chiefly confined to the towns and cities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Mexico">Southern Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Southern Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Southern Mexico had a large indigenous population and was geographically distant from the capital. Yucatán in particular had closer ties to Cuba and to the United States than it did to central Mexico. On a number of occasions in the early era of the Mexican Republic, Yucatán seceded from the federation. There were also rivalries between regional elites, with one faction based in Mérida and the other in Campeche. These issues factored into the Mexican–American War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S. Navy contributed to the war by controlling the coast and clearing the way for U.S. troops and supplies, especially to Mexico's main port of Veracruz. Even before hostilities began in the disputed northern region, the U.S. Navy created a blockade. Given the shallow waters of that portion of the Gulf coast, the U.S. Navy needed ships with a shallow draft rather than large frigates. Since the Mexican Navy was almost non-existent, the U.S. Navy could operate unimpeded in Gulf waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Battle_of_Tabasco">First Battle of Tabasco</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: First Battle of Tabasco">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Tabasco" title="First Battle of Tabasco">First Battle of Tabasco</a></div>
<p>Commodore <a href="/wiki/Matthew_C._Perry" title="Matthew C. Perry">Matthew C. Perry</a> led a detachment of seven vessels along the northern coast of <a href="/wiki/Tabasco" title="Tabasco">Tabasco</a> state. Perry arrived at the Tabasco River (now known as the <a href="/wiki/Grijalva_River" title="Grijalva River">Grijalva River</a>) on October 22, 1846, and seized the town Port of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Frontera,_Tabasco&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Frontera, Tabasco (page does not exist)">Frontera</a> along with two of their ships. Leaving a small garrison, he advanced with his troops towards the town of San Juan Bautista (<a href="/wiki/Villahermosa" title="Villahermosa">Villahermosa</a> today). Perry arrived in the city of San Juan Bautista on October 25, seizing five Mexican vessels. Colonel Juan Bautista Traconis, <a href="/wiki/Tabasco" title="Tabasco">Tabasco</a> Departmental commander at that time, set up barricades inside the buildings. Perry realized that the bombing of the city would be the only option to drive out the Mexican Army, and to avoid damage to the merchants of the city, withdrew its forces preparing them for the next day.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Intervenci%C3%B3n_estadounidense_en_Tabasco.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1885" data-file-width="2588" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Intervenci%C3%B3n_estadounidense_en_Tabasco.jpg/250px-Intervenci%C3%B3n_estadounidense_en_Tabasco.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Intervenci%C3%B3n_estadounidense_en_Tabasco.jpg/375px-Intervenci%C3%B3n_estadounidense_en_Tabasco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Intervenci%C3%B3n_estadounidense_en_Tabasco.jpg/500px-Intervenci%C3%B3n_estadounidense_en_Tabasco.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Intervenci%C3%B3n_estadounidense_en_Tabasco.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Tabasco" title="Second Battle of Tabasco">Second Battle of Tabasco</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the morning of October 26, as Perry's fleet prepared to start the attack on the city, the Mexican forces began firing at the American fleet. The U.S. bombing began to yield the square, so that the fire continued until evening. Before taking the square, Perry decided to leave and return to the port of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Frontera,_Tabasco&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Frontera, Tabasco (page does not exist)">Frontera</a>, where he established a naval blockade to prevent supplies of food and military supplies from reaching the state capital.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Battle_of_Tabasco">Second Battle of Tabasco</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Second Battle of Tabasco">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Tabasco" title="Second Battle of Tabasco">Second Battle of Tabasco</a></div>
<p>On June 13, 1847, Commodore Perry assembled the <a href="/wiki/Mosquito_Fleet" title="Mosquito Fleet">Mosquito Fleet</a> and began moving towards the <a href="/wiki/Grijalva_River" title="Grijalva River">Grijalva River</a>, towing 47 boats that carried a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landing_force" title="Landing force">landing force</a> of 1,173. On June 15, 12 miles (19 km) below San Juan Bautista, the fleet ran through an ambush with little difficulty. Again at an "S" curve in the river known as the "Devil's Bend", Perry encountered Mexican fire from a river fortification known as the Colmena redoubt, but the fleet's heavy naval guns quickly dispersed the Mexican force.</p>
<p>On June 16, Perry arrived at <a href="/wiki/Villahermosa" title="Villahermosa">San Juan Bautista</a> and commenced bombing the city. The attack included two ships that sailed past the fort and began shelling it from the rear. <a href="/wiki/David_Dixon_Porter" title="David Dixon Porter">David D. Porter</a> led 60 sailors ashore and seized the fort, raising the American flag over the works. Perry and the landing force arrived and took control of the city around 14:00.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yucat.C3.A1n">Yucatán</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Yucatán">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The U.S. was concerned with the extension of British power in the Caribbean, especially Spanish Cuba, as well as the strategic Yucatán peninsula. In 1847 Maya revolted against the white elites of the peninsula in a racial war known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caste_War_of_Yucatan" title="Caste War of Yucatan">Caste War of Yucatan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a>, then a senator from Mississippi, argued in congress that the president needed no further powers to intervene in Yucatan since the war with Mexico was underway. Davis's concern was strategic and part of his vision of Manifest Destiny, considering the Gulf of Mexico "a basin of water belonging to the United States" and continuing "the cape of Yucatan and the island of Cuba must be ours" rather than under British influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> In the end, the U.S. did not intervene in Yucatán, but it had figured in congressional debates about the Mexican–American War. At one point, the government of Yucatán petitioned the U.S. for protection during the Caste War,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> but the U.S. did not respond.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Desertion">Desertion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Desertion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Churubusco2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2414" data-file-width="3354" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Battle_of_Churubusco2.jpg/250px-Battle_of_Churubusco2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Battle_of_Churubusco2.jpg/375px-Battle_of_Churubusco2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Battle_of_Churubusco2.jpg/500px-Battle_of_Churubusco2.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Churubusco2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Churubusco" title="Battle of Churubusco">Battle of Churubusco</a></i> by J. Cameron, published by <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Currier" title="Nathaniel Currier">Nathaniel Currier</a>. Hand tinted lithograph, 1847. Digitally restored.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Battalion" title="Saint Patrick's Battalion">Saint Patrick's Battalion</a></div>
<p>Desertion was a major problem for the Mexican Army, depleting forces on the eve of battle. Most soldiers were peasants who had a loyalty to their village and family, but not to the generals who had conscripted them. Often hungry and ill, under-equipped, only partially trained, and never well paid, the soldiers were held in contempt by their officers and had little reason to fight the Americans. Looking for their opportunity, many slipped away from camp to find their way back to their home village.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>The desertion rate in the U.S. Army was 8.3% (9,200 out of 111,000), compared to 12.7% during the War of 1812 and usual peacetime rates of about 14.8% per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Many men deserted to join another U.S. unit and get a second enlistment bonus. Some deserted because of the miserable conditions in camp. It has been suggested that others used the army to get free transportation to California, where they deserted to join the gold rush;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foos_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-foos-145">[145]</a></sup> this, however, is unlikely as gold was only discovered in California on <a href="/wiki/California_Gold_Rush#History" title="California Gold Rush">January 24, 1848</a>, less than two weeks before the war concluded. By the time word reached the eastern U.S. that gold had been discovered, word also reached it that the war was over.</p>
<p>Several hundred U.S. deserters went over to the Mexican side. Nearly all were recent immigrants from Europe with weak ties to the U.S.; the most famous group was the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Battalion" title="Saint Patrick's Battalion">Saint Patrick's Battalion</a> composed primarily of <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German</a> immigrants, the battalion also included <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canadians</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Poles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_people" title="Spanish people">Spaniards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swiss_people" title="Swiss people">Swiss</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_people" title="Mexican people">Mexican people</a>, many of whom were members of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> The Mexicans issued broadsides and leaflets enticing U.S. soldiers with promises of money, land bounties, and officers' commissions. Mexican guerrillas shadowed the U.S. Army and captured men who took unauthorized leave or fell out of the ranks. The guerrillas coerced these men to join the Mexican ranks. The generous promises proved illusory for most deserters, who risked being executed if captured by U.S. forces. About 50 of the <i>San Patricios</i> were tried and hanged following their capture at Churubusco in August 1847.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foos_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-foos-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scott.27s_Mexico_City_campaign">Scott's Mexico City campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Scott's Mexico City campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
