<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Landings_and_siege_of_Veracruz">Landings and siege of Veracruz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Landings and siege of Veracruz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Veracruz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="640" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Battle_of_Veracruz.jpg/230px-Battle_of_Veracruz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Battle_of_Veracruz.jpg/345px-Battle_of_Veracruz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Battle_of_Veracruz.jpg/460px-Battle_of_Veracruz.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Veracruz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bombardment of Veracruz</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scott%27s_campaign-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="1411" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Scott%27s_campaign-en.svg/450px-Scott%27s_campaign-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Scott%27s_campaign-en.svg/675px-Scott%27s_campaign-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Scott%27s_campaign-en.svg/900px-Scott%27s_campaign-en.svg.png 2x" width="450"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scott%27s_campaign-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scott's campaign.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rather than reinforce Taylor's army for a continued advance, President Polk sent a second army under General <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>, which was transported to the port of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veracruz,_Veracruz" title="Veracruz, Veracruz">Veracruz</a> by sea, to begin an invasion of the Mexican heartland. On March 9, 1847, Scott performed the first major amphibious landing in U.S. history in preparation for the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Veracruz" title="Siege of Veracruz">Siege of Veracruz</a>. A group of 12,000 volunteer and regular soldiers successfully offloaded supplies, weapons, and horses near the walled city using specially designed landing craft. Included in the invading force were <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Meade" title="George Meade">George Meade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Longstreet" title="James Longstreet">James Longstreet</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_%22Stonewall%22_Jackson" title='Thomas "Stonewall" Jackson'>Thomas "Stonewall" Jackson</a>.</p>
<p>The city was defended by Mexican General Juan Morales with 3,400 men. <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(weapon)" title="Mortar (weapon)">Mortars</a> and naval guns under Commodore <a href="/wiki/Matthew_C._Perry" title="Matthew C. Perry">Matthew C. Perry</a> were used to reduce the city walls and harass defenders. After a bombardment on March 24, 1847, the walls of Veracruz had a thirty-foot gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The city replied the best it could with its own artillery. The effect of the extended barrage destroyed the will of the Mexican side to fight against a numerically superior force, and they surrendered the city after 12 days under siege. U.S. troops suffered 80 casualties, while the Mexican side had around 180 killed and wounded, about half of whom were civilian. During the siege, the U.S. side began to fall victim to <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Advance_on_Puebla">Advance on Puebla</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Advance on Puebla">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cerro_Gordo" title="Battle of Cerro Gordo">Battle of Cerro Gordo</a></div>
<p>Scott then marched westward on April 2, 1847, toward Mexico City with 8,500 healthy troops, while Santa Anna set up a defensive position in a canyon around the main road about 50 miles (80 km) north-west of Veracruz, near the hamlet of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cerro_Gordo" title="Battle of Cerro Gordo">Cerro Gordo</a>. Santa Anna had entrenched with 12,000 troops and artillery that were trained on the road, along which he expected Scott to appear. However, Scott had sent 2,600 mounted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dragoons" title="Dragoons">dragoons</a> ahead and they reached the pass on April 12. The Mexican artillery prematurely fired on them and therefore revealed their positions.</p>
<p>Instead of taking the main road, Scott's troops trekked through the rough terrain to the north, setting up his artillery on the high ground and quietly flanking the Mexicans. Although by then aware of the positions of U.S. troops, Santa Anna and his troops were unprepared for the onslaught that followed. In the battle fought on April 18, the Mexican army was routed. The U.S. Army suffered 400 casualties, while the Mexicans suffered over 1,000 casualties and 3,000 were taken prisoner. In August 1847, Captain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kirby_Smith" title="Kirby Smith">Kirby Smith</a>, of Scott's 3rd Infantry, reflected on the resistance of the Mexican army:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>They can do nothing and their continued defeats should convince them of it. They have lost six great battles; we have captured six hundred and eight cannon, nearly one hundred thousand stands of arms, made twenty thousand prisoners, have the greatest portion of their country and are fast advancing on their Capital which must be ours,—yet they refuse to treat [i.e., negotiate terms]!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoFar_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-SoFar-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pause_at_Puebla">Pause at Puebla</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Pause at Puebla">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In May, Scott pushed on to Puebla, the second largest city in Mexico. Because of the citizens' hostility to Santa Anna, the city capitulated without resistance on May 1. During the following months Scott gathered supplies and reinforcements at Puebla and sent back units whose enlistments had expired. Scott also made strong efforts to keep his troops disciplined and treat the Mexican people under occupation justly, so as to prevent a popular rising against his army.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Advance_on_Mexico_City_and_its_capture">Advance on Mexico City and its capture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Advance on Mexico City and its capture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nebel_Mexican_War_08_Molino_del_Rey_Molino.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3834" data-file-width="5263" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Nebel_Mexican_War_08_Molino_del_Rey_Molino.jpg/220px-Nebel_Mexican_War_08_Molino_del_Rey_Molino.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Nebel_Mexican_War_08_Molino_del_Rey_Molino.jpg/330px-Nebel_Mexican_War_08_Molino_del_Rey_Molino.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Nebel_Mexican_War_08_Molino_del_Rey_Molino.jpg/440px-Nebel_Mexican_War_08_Molino_del_Rey_Molino.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nebel_Mexican_War_08_Molino_del_Rey_Molino.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Molino_del_Rey" title="Battle of Molino del Rey">Battle of Molino del Rey</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With guerrillas harassing his line of communications back to Veracruz, Scott decided not to weaken his army to defend Puebla but, leaving only a garrison at Puebla to protect the sick and injured recovering there, advanced on Mexico City on August 7 with his remaining force. The capital was laid open in a series of battles around the right flank of the city defenses, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Contreras" title="Battle of Contreras">Battle of Contreras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Churubusco" title="Battle of Churubusco">Churubusco</a>, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chapultepec" title="Battle of Chapultepec">Battle of Chapultepec</a>. Fighting halted for a time when an armistice and peace negotiations followed the Battle of Churubusco, until they broke down, on September 6, 1847. With the subsequent battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Molino_del_Rey" title="Battle of Molino del Rey">Molino del Rey</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chapultepec" title="Battle of Chapultepec">Chapultepec</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_for_Mexico_City#Attacks_on_the_Bel.C3.A9n_and_San_Cosme_Gates" title="Battle for Mexico City">storming of the city gates</a>, the capital was occupied. <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a> became an American national hero after his victories in this campaign of the Mexican–American War, and later became military governor of occupied <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Chapultepec">Battle of Chapultepec</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Battle of Chapultepec">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chapultepec1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="984" data-file-width="1494" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Chapultepec1.jpg/220px-Chapultepec1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Chapultepec1.jpg/330px-Chapultepec1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Chapultepec1.jpg/440px-Chapultepec1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chapultepec1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Storming of Chapultepec</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Battle of Chapultepec was an encounter between the Mexican Army and the United States on the <a href="/wiki/Chapultepec_Castle" title="Chapultepec Castle">castle of Chapultepec</a> in Mexico City. At this time, this castle was a renowned military school in Mexico City. After the battle, which ended in an American victory, the legend of "Los Niños Héroes" was born. Although not confirmed by historians, six military cadets between the ages of 13 and 17 stayed in the school instead of evacuating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webtopia_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webtopia-149">[149]</a></sup> They decided to stay and fight for Mexico. These <a href="/wiki/Ni%C3%B1os_H%C3%A9roes" title="Niños Héroes">Niños Héroes</a> (hero children) became icons in Mexico's pantheon of heroes. Rather than surrender to the U.S. Army, some military cadets leaped from the castle walls. A cadet named Juan Escutia wrapped himself in the Mexican flag and jumped to his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webtopia_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Webtopia-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="San_Patricios">San Patricios</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: San Patricios">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sanpatricioshang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="377" data-file-width="550" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Sanpatricioshang.jpg/220px-Sanpatricioshang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Sanpatricioshang.jpg/330px-Sanpatricioshang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Sanpatricioshang.jpg/440px-Sanpatricioshang.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sanpatricioshang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The mass hanging of Irish Catholic soldiers who joined the Mexican side, forming the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Battalion" title="Saint Patrick's Battalion">Saint Patrick's Battalion</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Battalion" title="Saint Patrick's Battalion">Saint Patrick's Battalion</a> (<i>San Patricios</i>) was a group of several hundred immigrant soldiers, the majority <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Irish</a>, who deserted the U.S. Army because of ill-treatment or sympathetic leanings to fellow Mexican Catholics. They joined the Mexican army. Most were killed in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Churubusco" title="Battle of Churubusco">Battle of Churubusco</a>; about 100 were captured by the U.S. and roughly half were hanged as deserters. The leader, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jon_Riley" title="Jon Riley">Jon Riley</a>, was merely branded since he had deserted before the war started.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Santa_Anna.27s_last_campaign">Santa Anna's last campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Santa Anna's last campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In late September 1847, Santa Anna made one last attempt to defeat the Americans, by cutting them off from the coast. General <a href="/wiki/Joaqu%C3%ADn_Rea" title="Joaquín Rea">Joaquín Rea</a> began the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Puebla_(1847)" title="Siege of Puebla (1847)">Siege of Puebla</a>, soon joined by Santa Anna, but they failed to take it before the approach of a relief column from Veracruz under Brig. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lane" title="Joseph Lane">Joseph Lane</a> prompted Santa Anna to stop him. Puebla was relieved by Gen. Lane October 12, 1847, following his defeat of Santa Anna at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Huamantla" title="Battle of Huamantla">Battle of Huamantla</a> on October 9, 1847. The battle was Santa Anna's last. Following the defeat, the new Mexican government led by <a href="/wiki/Manuel_de_la_Pe%C3%B1a_y_Pe%C3%B1a" title="Manuel de la Peña y Peña">Manuel de la Peña y Peña</a> asked Santa Anna to turn over command of the army to General <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Joaqu%C3%ADn_de_Herrera" title="José Joaquín de Herrera">José Joaquín de Herrera</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti_guerrilla_campaign">Anti guerrilla campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Anti guerrilla campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following his capture and securing of the capital, General Scott sent about a quarter of his strength to secure his line of communications to Vera Cruz from the Light Corps of General <a href="/wiki/Joaqu%C3%ADn_Rea" title="Joaquín Rea">Joaquín Rea</a> and other Mexican <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guerrilla" title="Guerrilla">guerrilla</a> forces that had been harassing it since May. He strengthened the garrison of Puebla and by November had added a 1200-man garrison at <a href="/wiki/Xalapa" title="Xalapa">Jalapa</a>, established 750-man posts along the National Road, the main route between the port of Veracruz and the capital, at the pass between Mexico City and Puebla at <a href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Fr%C3%ADo_de_Ju%C3%A1rez" title="Río Frío de Juárez">Rio Frio</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Perote,_Veracruz" title="Perote, Veracruz">Perote</a> and <a href="/wiki/Las_Vigas_de_Ram%C3%ADrez,_Veracruz" title="Las Vigas de Ramírez, Veracruz">San Juan</a> on the road between Jalapa and Puebla and at <a href="/wiki/Puente_Nacional,_Veracruz" title="Puente Nacional, Veracruz">Puente Nacional</a> between Jalapa and Vera Cruz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> He had also detailed an anti guerrilla brigade under Brig. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lane" title="Joseph Lane">Joseph Lane</a> to carry the war to the Light Corps and other guerrillas. He ordered that convoys would travel with at least 1,300-man escorts. Victories by General Lane over the Light Corps at <a href="/wiki/Atlixco" title="Atlixco">Atlixco</a> (October 18, 1847), at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Izucar_de_Matamoros" title="Izucar de Matamoros">Izucar de Matamoros</a> (November 23, 1847) and <a href="/wiki/Affair_at_Galaxara_Pass" title="Affair at Galaxara Pass">Galaxara Pass</a> (November 24, 1847) ended the threat of General Rea.</p>
<p>Later a raid against the guerrillas of Padre <a href="/wiki/Celedonio_D%C3%B3meco_de_Jarauta" title="Celedonio Dómeco de Jarauta">Jarauta</a> at <a href="/wiki/Zacualtipan" title="Zacualtipan">Zacualtipan</a> (February 25, 1848), further reduced guerrilla raids on the American line of communications. After the two governments concluded a truce to await ratification of the peace treaty, on March 6, 1848, formal hostilities ceased. However some bands continued in defiance of the Mexican government until the American evacuation in August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Some were suppressed by the Mexican Army or, like Padre Jarauta, executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo">Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:303px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wpdms_mexican_cession.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="265" data-file-width="301" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/44/Wpdms_mexican_cession.png" width="301"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wpdms_mexican_cession.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Cession" title="Mexican Cession">Mexican Cession</a>, shown in red, and the later <a href="/wiki/Gadsden_Purchase" title="Gadsden Purchase">Gadsden Purchase</a>, shown in yellow.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Outnumbered militarily and with many of its large cities occupied, Mexico could not defend itself; the country was also faced with many internal divisions, including the <a href="/wiki/Caste_War_of_Yucat%C3%A1n" title="Caste War of Yucatán">Caste War of Yucatán</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</a>, signed on February 2, 1848, by American diplomat <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Trist" title="Nicholas Trist">Nicholas Trist</a> and Mexican <a href="/wiki/Plenipotentiary" title="Plenipotentiary">plenipotentiary</a> representatives Luis G. Cuevas, Bernardo Couto, and Miguel Atristain, ended the war. The treaty gave the U.S. undisputed control of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, established the U.S.-Mexican border of the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande" title="Rio Grande">Rio Grande</a>, and ceded to the United States the present-day states of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>. In return, Mexico received <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">$</a>15 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> ($415 million today) – less than half the amount the U.S. had attempted to offer Mexico for the land before the opening of hostilities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> – and the U.S. agreed to assume $3.25 million ($90 million today) in debts that the Mexican government owed to U.S. citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>The acquisition was a source of controversy then, especially among U.S. politicians who had opposed the war from the start. A leading antiwar U.S. newspaper, the <i>Whig Intelligencer</i>, sardonically concluded that "We take nothing by conquest .... Thank God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mexican_Cession_in_Mexican_View.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="941" data-file-width="1068" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Mexican_Cession_in_Mexican_View.PNG/350px-Mexican_Cession_in_Mexican_View.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Mexican_Cession_in_Mexican_View.PNG/525px-Mexican_Cession_in_Mexican_View.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Mexican_Cession_in_Mexican_View.PNG/700px-Mexican_Cession_in_Mexican_View.PNG 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mexican_Cession_in_Mexican_View.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mexican territorial claims relinquished in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</a> in white.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a> introduced an amendment giving the U.S. most of <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Mexico" title="Northeastern Mexico">northeastern Mexico</a>, which failed 44–11. It was supported by both senators from Texas (<a href="/wiki/Sam_Houston" title="Sam Houston">Sam Houston</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Rusk" title="Thomas Jefferson Rusk">Thomas Jefferson Rusk</a>), <a href="/wiki/Daniel_S._Dickinson" title="Daniel S. Dickinson">Daniel S. Dickinson</a> of New York, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> of Illinois, <a href="/wiki/Edward_A._Hannegan" title="Edward A. Hannegan">Edward A. Hannegan</a> of Indiana, and one each from Alabama, Florida, Mississippi, Ohio, Missouri, and Tennessee. Most of the leaders of the Democratic party – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Hart_Benton_(senator)" title="Thomas Hart Benton (senator)">Thomas Hart Benton</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herschel_V._Johnson" title="Herschel V. Johnson">Herschel V. Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Cass" title="Lewis Cass">Lewis Cass</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Murray_Mason" title="James Murray Mason">James Murray Mason</a> of Virginia, and <a href="/wiki/Ambrose_Hundley_Sevier" title="Ambrose Hundley Sevier">Ambrose Hundley Sevier</a> – were opposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> An amendment by Whig Senator <a href="/wiki/George_Edmund_Badger" title="George Edmund Badger">George Edmund Badger</a> of North Carolina to exclude New Mexico and Upper California lost 35–15, with three Southern Whigs voting with the Democrats. <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a> was bitter that four New England senators made deciding votes for acquiring the new territories.</p>
<p>The acquired lands west of the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande" title="Rio Grande">Rio Grande</a> are traditionally called the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Cession" title="Mexican Cession">Mexican Cession</a> in the U.S., as opposed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Texas_Annexation" title="Texas Annexation">Texas Annexation</a> two years earlier, though division of <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> down the middle at the Rio Grande never had any basis either in control or Mexican boundaries. Mexico never recognized the independence of Texas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> before the war, and did not cede its claim to territory north of the Rio Grande or <a href="/wiki/Gila_River" title="Gila River">Gila River</a> until this treaty.</p>
<p>Before ratifying the treaty the U.S. Senate made two modifications: changing the wording of Article IX (which guaranteed Mexicans living in the purchased territories the right to become U.S. citizens) and striking out Article X (which conceded the legitimacy of land grants made by the Mexican government). On May 26, 1848, when the two countries exchanged ratifications of the treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo, they further agreed to a three-article protocol (known as the Protocol of Querétaro) to explain the amendments. The first article claimed that the original Article IX of the treaty, although replaced by Article III of the Treaty of Louisiana, would still confer the rights delineated in Article IX. The second article confirmed the legitimacy of land grants under Mexican law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hidalgo_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-hidalgo-162">[162]</a></sup> The protocol was signed in the city of Querétaro by A. H. Sevier, <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Clifford" title="Nathan Clifford">Nathan Clifford</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_la_Rosa_Oteiza" title="Luis de la Rosa Oteiza">Luis de la Rosa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hidalgo_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-hidalgo-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Article XI offered a potential benefit to Mexico, in that the US pledged to suppress the Comanche and Apache raids that had ravaged northern Mexico and pay restitutions to the victims of raids it could not prevent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> However, the Indian raids did not cease for several decades after the treaty, although a cholera epidemic reduced the numbers of the Comanche in 1849.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Letcher" title="Robert Letcher">Robert Letcher</a>, U.S. Minister to Mexico in 1850, was certain "that miserable 11th article" would lead to the financial ruin of the US if it could not be released from its obligations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> The US was released from all obligations of Article XI five years later by Article II of the <a href="/wiki/Gadsden_Purchase" title="Gadsden Purchase">Gadsden Purchase</a> of 1853.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nebel_Mexican_War_12_Scott_in_Mexico_City.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3893" data-file-width="5237" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Nebel_Mexican_War_12_Scott_in_Mexico_City.jpg/250px-Nebel_Mexican_War_12_Scott_in_Mexico_City.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Nebel_Mexican_War_12_Scott_in_Mexico_City.jpg/375px-Nebel_Mexican_War_12_Scott_in_Mexico_City.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Nebel_Mexican_War_12_Scott_in_Mexico_City.jpg/500px-Nebel_Mexican_War_12_Scott_in_Mexico_City.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nebel_Mexican_War_12_Scott_in_Mexico_City.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
American occupation of <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Altered_territories">Altered territories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Altered territories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before the secession of Texas, Mexico comprised almost 1,700,000 sq mi (4,400,000 km<sup>2</sup>), but by 1849 it was just under 800,000 square miles (2,100,000 km<sup>2</sup>). Another 30,000 square miles (78,000 km<sup>2</sup>) were sold to the U.S. in the <a href="/wiki/Gadsden_Purchase" title="Gadsden Purchase">Gadsden Purchase</a> of 1853, so the total reduction of Mexican territory was more than 55%, or 900,000 square miles (2,300,000 km<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though the annexed territory was about the size of Western Europe, it was sparsely populated. The land contained about 14,000 non-indigenous people in <a href="/wiki/Alta_California" title="Alta California">Alta California</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> and about 60,000 in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_de_Nuevo_M%C3%A9xico" title="Santa Fe de Nuevo México">Nuevo México</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> as well as large <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indian</a> nations, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tohono_O%27odham" title="Tohono O'odham">Papago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pima_people" title="Pima people">Pima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puebloan_peoples" title="Puebloan peoples">Puebloan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navajo_people" title="Navajo people">Navajo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apache" title="Apache">Apache</a> and many others. Although some native people relocated farther south in Mexico, the great majority remained in the U.S. territory.</p>
<p>The American settlers surging into the newly conquered Southwest were openly contemptuous of Mexican law (a <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law system</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Spain" title="Law of Spain">law of Spain</a>) as alien and inferior and disposed of it by enacting <a href="/wiki/Reception_statute" title="Reception statute">reception statutes</a> at the first available opportunity. However, they recognized the value of a few aspects of Mexican law and carried them over into their new legal systems. For example, most of the southwestern states adopted <a href="/wiki/Community_property" title="Community property">community property</a> marital property systems, as well as water law.</p>
<p>Mexicans and Indians in the annexed territories faced a loss of civil and political rights, even though the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</a> promised American citizenship to all Mexican citizens living in the territory of the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Cession" title="Mexican Cession">Mexican Cession</a>. The U.S. government withheld citizenship from Indians in the southwest until the 1930s, despite the fact that they were citizens under Mexican law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_the_war_in_the_United_States">Impact of the war in the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Impact of the war in the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In much of the United States of America, victory and the acquisition of new land brought a surge of patriotism. Victory seemed to fulfill Democrats' belief in their country's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manifest_Destiny" title="Manifest Destiny">Manifest Destiny</a>. While Whig <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a> rejected war "as a means of achieving America's destiny," he accepted that "most of the great results of history are brought about by discreditable means."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Although the Whigs had opposed the war, they made Zachary Taylor their presidential candidate in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1848" title="United States presidential election, 1848">election of 1848</a>, praising his military performance while muting their criticism of the war.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Has the Mexican War terminated yet, and how? Are we beaten? Do you know of any nation about to besiege South Hadley [Massachusetts]? If so, do inform me of it, for I would be glad of a chance to escape, if we are to be stormed. I suppose [our teacher] Miss <a href="/wiki/Mary_Lyon" title="Mary Lyon">[Mary] Lyon</a> would furnish us all with daggers and order us to fight for our lives...</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— The sixteen-year-old <a href="/wiki/Emily_Dickinson" title="Emily Dickinson">Emily Dickinson</a>, writing to her older brother, <a href="/wiki/William_Austin_Dickinson" title="William Austin Dickinson">Austin</a> in the fall of 1847, shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chapultepec" title="Battle of Chapultepec">Battle of Chapultepec</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>A month before the end of the war, Polk was criticized in a <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> amendment to a bill praising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_General" title="Major General">Major General</a> <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a> for "a war unnecessarily and unconstitutionally begun by the President of the United States." This criticism, in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congressman" title="Congressman">Congressman</a> <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> played an important role with his <a href="/wiki/Spot_Resolutions" title="Spot Resolutions">Spot Resolutions</a>, followed congressional scrutiny of the war's beginnings, including factual challenges to claims made by President Polk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> The vote followed party lines, with all Whigs supporting the amendment. Lincoln's attack won luke-warm support from fellow Whigs in <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> but was harshly counter-attacked by Democrats, who rallied pro-war sentiments in Illinois; Lincoln's Spot resolutions haunted his future campaigns in the heavily Democratic state of Illinois, and were cited by enemies well into his presidency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_the_American_Civil_War">Effect on the American Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Effect on the American Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg"><img alt=" Engraving of young Grant in uniform" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1586" data-file-width="1122" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg/220px-Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg/330px-Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg/440px-Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brevet_Second_Lieutenant_Ulysses_S._Grant_in_1843.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Second lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, one of the many officers in the U.S. Army in the Mexican–American War to serve in the American Civil War</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many of the military leaders on both sides of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> were trained at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Military_Academy" title="U.S. Military Academy">U.S. Military Academy</a> at West Point and had fought as junior officers in Mexico. This list includes military men fighting for the Union: <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_B._McClellan" title="George B. McClellan">George B. McClellan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_T._Sherman" title="William T. Sherman">William T. Sherman</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Meade" title="George Meade">George Meade</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Rosecrans" title="William Rosecrans">William Rosecrans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ambrose_Burnside" title="Ambrose Burnside">Ambrose Burnside</a>. Military men who joined the Southern secessionists of the Confederate States of America were <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stonewall_Jackson" title="Stonewall Jackson">Stonewall Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Longstreet" title="James Longstreet">James Longstreet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_E._Johnston" title="Joseph E. Johnston">Joseph E. Johnston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Braxton_Bragg" title="Braxton Bragg">Braxton Bragg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Price" title="Sterling Price">Sterling Price</a>, and the future <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> President <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a>. Both sides had leaders with significant experience in active combat in strategy and tactics, likely shaping ways the Civil War conflict played out.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, who as a young <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">army</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Lieutenant_(United_States)" title="Second Lieutenant (United States)">lieutenant</a> had served in Mexico under General Taylor, recalled in his <i><a href="/wiki/Personal_Memoirs_of_Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Personal Memoirs of Ulysses S. Grant">Memoirs</a></i>, published in 1885, that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Generally, the officers of the army were indifferent whether the annexation was consummated or not; but not so all of them. For myself, I was bitterly opposed to the measure, and to this day regard the war, which resulted, as one of the most unjust ever waged by a stronger against a weaker nation. It was an instance of a republic following the bad example of European monarchies, in not considering justice in their desire to acquire additional territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Grant also expressed the view that the war against Mexico had brought punishment on the United States in the form of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Southern rebellion was largely the outgrowth of the Mexican war. Nations, like individuals, are punished for their transgressions. We got our punishment in the most sanguinary and expensive war of modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>This view was shared by the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a>, who towards the end of the war wrote that "The United States will conquer Mexico, but it will be as the man swallows the arsenic, which brings him down in turn. Mexico will poison us."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whig_primary_1848c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3804" data-file-width="2622" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Whig_primary_1848c.jpg/170px-Whig_primary_1848c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Whig_primary_1848c.jpg/255px-Whig_primary_1848c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Whig_primary_1848c.jpg/340px-Whig_primary_1848c.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whig_primary_1848c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"An Available Candidate: The One Qualification for a Whig President." Political cartoon about the 1848 presidential election which refers to <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a> or <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>, the two leading contenders for the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a> nomination in the aftermath of the Mexican–American War. Published by <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Currier" title="Nathaniel Currier">Nathaniel Currier</a> in 1848, digitally restored.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>General Robert E. Lee, leader of the confederate forces through the end of the American Civil War, began building his reputation as a military officer in America's war against Mexico. At the start of the Mexican–American War, Captain Lee invaded Mexico with General Wool's engineering department from the North. By early 1847, he helped take the Mexican cities of Vera Cruz, Quebec, Cerro Gordo, Contreras, Churubusco, Molino del Rey and Chapultepec. Lee was wounded in Chapultepec. By September, Mexico City surrendered and the United States was victorious.</p>
<p>General Scott was the ranking officer in the army during the Mexican–American campaign. He described Robert E. Lee as "gallant and indefatigable," saying that Lee had displayed the "greatest feat of physical and moral courage performed by any individual in [his] knowledge during the campaign." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-179">[179]</a></sup> Robert E. Lee's humility and professionalism was apparent early on in his career when gave credit to General Scott for the victories. He said that "It was his stout heart…his bold self reliance…his indomitable courage that…ressed us forward to this capital." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-179">[179]</a></sup> It is important to note that although Lee is remembered for his valor during the Mexican–American War, he was only a junior officer "who had never commanded a regiment in the field" <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_179-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1861, it was General Scott who advised Abraham Lincoln to ask Lee to Command the union forces. Lee declined, and later recounted "I declined the offer he made me to take command of the army that was brought into the field, stating candidly and as courteously as I could that though opposed to secession and depreciating war, I could take no part in the invasion of the southern states."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> (Sneiderman,118) On 9 April 1865, it was General Robert E. Lee who had surrendered to President Lincoln's Union Forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite initial objections from the Whigs and abolitionists, the war would nevertheless unite the U.S. in a common cause and was fought almost entirely by volunteers. The army swelled from just over 6,000 to more than 115,000. The majority of 12-month volunteers in Scott's army decided that a year's fighting was enough and returned to the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>Veterans of the war were often broken men. "As the sick and wounded from Taylor's and Scott's campaigns made their way back from Mexico to the United States, their condition shocked the folks at home. Husbands, sons, and brothers returned in broken health, some with missing limbs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>For years afterward, U.S. veterans continued to suffer from the debilitating diseases contracted during the campaigns. The casualty rate was thus easily over 25% for the 17 months of the war; the total casualties may have reached 35–40% if later injury- and disease-related deaths are added.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In this respect, the war was proportionately the most deadly in American military history.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Overall, approximately 1.5% of U.S. soldiers were killed in the fighting and nearly 10% died of disease; another 12% were wounded or discharged because of disease, or both.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>During the war, political quarrels in the U.S. arose regarding the disposition of conquered Mexico. A brief <a href="/wiki/All_of_Mexico_Movement" title="All of Mexico Movement">"All-Mexico" movement</a> urged annexation of the entire territory. Veterans of the war who had seen Mexico at first hand were unenthusiastic.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Anti-slavery elements opposed that position and fought for the exclusion of slavery from any territory absorbed by the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> In 1847 the House of Representatives passed the <a href="/wiki/Wilmot_Proviso" title="Wilmot Proviso">Wilmot Proviso</a>, stipulating that none of the territory acquired should be open to slavery. The Senate avoided the issue, and a late attempt to add it to the Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo was defeated.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Guadalupe_Hidalgo" title="Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo">Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo</a> was the result of <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Trist" title="Nicholas Trist">Nicholas Trist</a>'s unauthorized negotiations. It was approved by the U.S. Senate on March 10, 1848, and ratified by the Mexican Congress on May 25. Mexico's cession of <a href="/wiki/Alta_California" title="Alta California">Alta California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_de_Nuevo_M%C3%A9xico" title="Santa Fe de Nuevo México">Nuevo México</a> and its recognition of U.S. sovereignty over all of Texas north of the Rio Grande formalized the addition of 1.2 million square miles (3.1 million km<sup>2</sup>) of territory to the United States. In return the U.S. agreed to pay $15 million and assumed the claims of its citizens against Mexico. A final territorial adjustment between Mexico and the U.S. was made by the <a href="/wiki/Gadsden_Purchase" title="Gadsden Purchase">Gadsden Purchase</a> in 1853. The sale of this territory was a contributing factor in the final fall of Santa Anna in Mexico for having sold Mexican patrimony.</p>
<p>As late as 1880, the "Republican Campaign Textbook" by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Congressional_Committee&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Republican Congressional Committee (page does not exist)">Republican Congressional Committee</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> described the war as "Feculent, reeking Corruption" and "one of the darkest scenes in our history—a war forced upon our and the Mexican people by the high-handed usurpations of Pres't Polk in pursuit of territorial aggrandizement of the slave oligarchy."</p>
<p>The war was one of the most decisive events for the U.S. in the first half of the 19th century. While it marked a significant waypoint for the nation as a growing military power, it also served as a milestone especially within the U.S. narrative of Manifest Destiny. The resultant territorial gains set in motion many of the defining trends in American 19th-century history, particularly for the American West. The war did not resolve the issue of slavery in the U.S. but rather in many ways inflamed it, as potential westward expansion of the institution took an increasingly central and heated theme in national debates preceding the American Civil War. Furthermore, in doing much to extend the nation from coast to coast, the Mexican–American War was one step in the massive migrations to the West of Americans, which culminated in transcontinental railroads and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_wars" title="Indian wars">Indian wars</a> later in the same century.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_the_war_in_Mexico">Impact of the war in Mexico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mexican%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Impact of the war in Mexico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lula_in_Chapultepec.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1654" data-file-width="2480" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Lula_in_Chapultepec.jpg/220px-Lula_in_Chapultepec.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Lula_in_Chapultepec.jpg/330px-Lula_in_Chapultepec.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Lula_in_Chapultepec.jpg/440px-Lula_in_Chapultepec.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lula_in_Chapultepec.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Memorial to the Mexican cadets killed in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chapultepec" title="Battle of Chapultepec">Battle of Chapultepec</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The obvious impact of the war was the loss of its northern areas and the defeat of the Mexican military. The war remains a painful historical event for Mexicans. In the immediate aftermath of the war, a group of prominent Mexicans wrote an assessment of the reasons for the war and Mexico's defeat, edited by <a href="/wiki/Ram%C3%B3n_Alcaraz" title="Ramón Alcaraz">Ramón Alcaraz</a> and including contributions by <a href="/wiki/Ignacio_Ram%C3%ADrez" title="Ignacio Ramírez">Ignacio Ramírez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guillermo_Prieto" title="Guillermo Prieto">Guillermo Prieto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Iglesias" title="José María Iglesias">José María Iglesias</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francisco_Urquidi" title="Francisco Urquidi">Francisco Urquidi</a>. The work was translated to English by Col. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Ramsey" title="Albert Ramsey">Albert Ramsey</a>, a veteran of the Mexican–American War and published in 1850.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Mexico City's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chapultepec_Park" title="Chapultepec Park">Chapultepec Park</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Ni%C3%B1os_H%C3%A9roes" title="Niños Héroes">Niños Héroes</a></i> (Monument to the Heroic Cadets) commemorates the heroic sacrifice of six teenaged military cadets who fought to their deaths rather than surrender to American troops during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chapultepec" title="Battle of Chapultepec">Battle of Chapultepec</a> Castle on September 13, 1847. The monument is an important patriotic site in Mexico. On March 5, 1947, nearly one hundred years after the battle, U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> placed a wreath at the monument and stood for a <a href="/wiki/Moment_of_silence" title="Moment of silence">moment of silence</a>.</p>
